 <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
          

          
     
                          <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Brown in Germany</h1>






<div class="clear-block">


<article id="node-288" class="main-article node-page node">








  <div class="content">
        <p><span class="thumbnail-wrapper inline inline-left" style="width:203px;"><img src="https://www.brown.edu/academics/college/special-programs/international-study/sites/brown.edu.academics.college.special-programs.international-study/files/images/brandenburg-gate-275437_0.preview.jpg" alt="" title=""  class="image image-preview " width="195" height="146" /></span><strong>At a Glance</strong><br />City: Berlin<br />Partner: <a href="http://www.hu-berlin.de/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">Humboldt University</a><br />Housing: University student housing or independent shared apartment with local German students<br />Language Requirements: <span>Third-semester college-level German for the Intermediate track or f</span>ifth-semester college-level German for the Advanced track (at Brown&nbsp;<a href="http://brown.edu/academics/college/special-programs/international-study/student-resources/getting-started/language-requirements#german">GRMN 0300 or GRMN 0500</a>)<br />Calendar: Full Year or Spring semester<br />Accepts Non-Brown Students: Yes</p>
<h3>Overview</h3>
<p>Imagine yourself in&nbsp;the lively capital city of Berlin, where you will find yourself immersed in&nbsp;Germany’s rich culture and complex history.&nbsp;Continuing to grapple with the effects of the 1989 reunification while experiencing steady growth of its ethnic minority population, Berlin offers an ideal venue to study the changing face of present-day Europe and a fascinating glimpse into&nbsp;Germany’s past, present and future.</p>
<h3>Orientation</h3>
<p>Brown's Berlin program begins a month-long orientation that includes a credit-bearing intensive German language course, cultural activities, and visits to different areas of the city.&nbsp;</p>
<p><em>Intensive German as a Foreign Language Course</em></p>
<p>All students enroll in an intensive month-long <a href="https://www.sprachenzentrum.hu-berlin.de/en/erasmus-deutsch-intensivkurse-en/march" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">German language course</a> offered by Humboldt University prior to the start of the regular semester courses. Students who successfully complete this course earn 7 <span>European Credit Transfer System (ECTS) credits, which is the equivalent of one Brown course credit.&nbsp;</span></p>
<p>The Advanced level track is a full immersion experience in which students take all of their courses in German at Humboldt  University. &nbsp;<span>Students in the Intermediate track may take advanced level courses in English at Humboldt University. Intermediate level students are required to enroll in a German language course during their regular semester, in additon to the intensive language course during orientation. &nbsp;</span></p>
<h3>Academics</h3>
<p>Following orientation, students enroll in regular semester courses at Humboldt University. Students must complete a minimum of &nbsp;20 - 23 ECTS&nbsp;<span>for evaluation through a <em>schein (</em>a document confirming that you have completed the course and received a letter grade), which, along with the intensive language course, would allow them to obtain a full semester credit equivalent at Brown.&nbsp;</span>Students may choose from Proseminare, Seminare (junior/senior level courses) or  Übungen (lab courses with field trips). Students are encouraged to  attend Vorlesungen (lecture courses in which there is no assessed work).&nbsp; Students may take a maximum of two Vorlesungen and may opt to enroll in one or both for credit. Students who wish to earn credit must arrange with the professor to be  given work on which a grade can be assessed. Please visit the <a href="https://www.hu-berlin.de/en/studies/counselling/course-catalogue" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">Humboldt University course catalog</a> for the most current list of course offerings.</p>
<p>Past program participants have studied history, art history, international studies, political science, religion, German, Medical Studies, natural sciences, chemistry, physics, computer science, comparative literarture, and international relations<span></span>. Students may enroll  in all departments of the universities for which they have appropriate  academic preparation.&nbsp;</p>
<p>In addition to academic programs, students have pursued internships at art galleries, the Aspen Institute, and the American embassy.&nbsp;</p>
<p><strong>Humboldt University</strong></p>
<p>Founded in 1810 by the Prussian king, Friedrich Wilhelm III, <a href="https://www.hu-berlin.de/en?set_language=en" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">Humboldt University</a>'s mission has long been to achieve a unity of teaching and research and provide students with an all-around humanist education. By drawing on and contributing to the rich history and cultural life of the city, Humboldt has remained true to its principles and become an internationally acclaimed institution.</p>
<p><strong>Academic Calendar</strong><br />Participants may enroll for either the spring semester or the full year.&nbsp;<span>Approximate dates are provided based on past programs; students should expect them to vary by year. &nbsp;Please refer to the&nbsp;</span><span><a href="https://www.brown.edu/academics/college/special-programs/international-study/sites/brown.edu.academics.college.special-programs.international-study/files/uploads/Berlin%20Program%20Calendar%20Spring%202017_0.pdf" target="_blank">program calendar</a></span><span>&nbsp;for specific information about program dates.</span><strong>&nbsp;</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Full Year: September through late July/early August</li>
<li>Spring Semester Only: March through late July/early August</li>
</ul>
<h3>Housing</h3>
<p>Students may choose to apply for <a href="https://www.international.hu- berlin.de/en/studierende/aus-dem-ausland/accommodation-1?set_language=en" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">student university housing</a> through Humboldt University or live in a shared apartment with local German students. Students who opt for shared apartment living are initially housed in temporary housing arranged by the program for the orientation period.  Throughout the orientation period, students work with the on-site coordinator and a local housing agency to find a room in a shared  apartment, where they will live with local Germans for the  remainder of the semester or year.</p>
<h3>Eligibility and Requirements</h3>
<p><span>The Brown in Germany program in Berlin is open to Brown and visiting undergraduate students. &nbsp;</span></p>
<p><span></span>Students must be in good academic standing. &nbsp;Students must have completed Intermediate German I/GRMN 0300 (third-semester college-level German), or the equivalent (Intermediate level track) or Twentieth-Century German Culture/GRMN 0500 (fifth-semester college-level German language), or the equivalent (Advanced level track) before beginning studies in Germany. &nbsp;</p>
<h3>Application Instructions</h3>
<p>Apply by the<a href="http://www.brown.edu/academics/college/special-programs/international-study/student-resources/getting-started/application-deadlines" target="_blank"> deadline </a>using   Brown's online application system via the "apply now" and "return to   application" buttons on the right side of this page.&nbsp; Applications   typically require faculty recommendations so it is important to begin   the process early.</p>

				<h1><a href="/academics/german-studies/" title="German Studies" accesskey="2"><span class="site-title">German Studies</span></a></h1><div id="site-slogan" class="slogan banner-subtext"></div>			</div></section>

        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Courses in German Studies</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
                                                  <div class="clear-block"> 
<article id="node-215" class="main-article node">








  <div class="content">
        <p>The Department of German Studies offers a set of courses that covers the range from beginning language instruction to graduate seminars.</p>
<p>You can select either "Fall" or "Spring" in the navigation menu to the left to see our current offerings.</p>
<p>Alternatively, you can download PDFs of both current and past offerings here:</p>

 <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
          

          
     
                          <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Brown in Germany</h1>






<div class="clear-block">


<article id="node-288" class="main-article node-page node">








  <div class="content">
        <p><span class="thumbnail-wrapper inline inline-left" style="width:203px;"><img src="https://www.brown.edu/academics/college/special-programs/international-study/sites/brown.edu.academics.college.special-programs.international-study/files/images/brandenburg-gate-275437_0.preview.jpg" alt="" title=""  class="image image-preview " width="195" height="146" /></span><strong>At a Glance</strong><br />City: Berlin<br />Partner: <a href="http://www.hu-berlin.de/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">Humboldt University</a><br />Housing: University student housing or independent shared apartment with local German students<br />Language Requirements: <span>Third-semester college-level German for the Intermediate track or f</span>ifth-semester college-level German for the Advanced track (at Brown&nbsp;<a href="http://brown.edu/academics/college/special-programs/international-study/student-resources/getting-started/language-requirements#german">GRMN 0300 or GRMN 0500</a>)<br />Calendar: Full Year or Spring semester<br />Accepts Non-Brown Students: Yes</p>
<h3>Overview</h3>
<p>Imagine yourself in&nbsp;the lively capital city of Berlin, where you will find yourself immersed in&nbsp;Germany’s rich culture and complex history.&nbsp;Continuing to grapple with the effects of the 1989 reunification while experiencing steady growth of its ethnic minority population, Berlin offers an ideal venue to study the changing face of present-day Europe and a fascinating glimpse into&nbsp;Germany’s past, present and future.</p>
<h3>Orientation</h3>
<p>Brown's Berlin program begins a month-long orientation that includes a credit-bearing intensive German language course, cultural activities, and visits to different areas of the city.&nbsp;</p>
<p><em>Intensive German as a Foreign Language Course</em></p>
<p>All students enroll in an intensive month-long <a href="https://www.sprachenzentrum.hu-berlin.de/en/erasmus-deutsch-intensivkurse-en/march" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">German language course</a> offered by Humboldt University prior to the start of the regular semester courses. Students who successfully complete this course earn 7 <span>European Credit Transfer System (ECTS) credits, which is the equivalent of one Brown course credit.&nbsp;</span></p>
<p>The Advanced level track is a full immersion experience in which students take all of their courses in German at Humboldt  University. &nbsp;<span>Students in the Intermediate track may take advanced level courses in English at Humboldt University. Intermediate level students are required to enroll in a German language course during their regular semester, in additon to the intensive language course during orientation. &nbsp;</span></p>
<h3>Academics</h3>
<p>Following orientation, students enroll in regular semester courses at Humboldt University. Students must complete a minimum of &nbsp;20 - 23 ECTS&nbsp;<span>for evaluation through a <em>schein (</em>a document confirming that you have completed the course and received a letter grade), which, along with the intensive language course, would allow them to obtain a full semester credit equivalent at Brown.&nbsp;</span>Students may choose from Proseminare, Seminare (junior/senior level courses) or  Übungen (lab courses with field trips). Students are encouraged to  attend Vorlesungen (lecture courses in which there is no assessed work).&nbsp; Students may take a maximum of two Vorlesungen and may opt to enroll in one or both for credit. Students who wish to earn credit must arrange with the professor to be  given work on which a grade can be assessed. Please visit the <a href="https://www.hu-berlin.de/en/studies/counselling/course-catalogue" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">Humboldt University course catalog</a> for the most current list of course offerings.</p>
<p>Past program participants have studied history, art history, international studies, political science, religion, German, Medical Studies, natural sciences, chemistry, physics, computer science, comparative literarture, and international relations<span></span>. Students may enroll  in all departments of the universities for which they have appropriate  academic preparation.&nbsp;</p>
<p>In addition to academic programs, students have pursued internships at art galleries, the Aspen Institute, and the American embassy.&nbsp;</p>
<p><strong>Humboldt University</strong></p>
<p>Founded in 1810 by the Prussian king, Friedrich Wilhelm III, <a href="https://www.hu-berlin.de/en?set_language=en" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">Humboldt University</a>'s mission has long been to achieve a unity of teaching and research and provide students with an all-around humanist education. By drawing on and contributing to the rich history and cultural life of the city, Humboldt has remained true to its principles and become an internationally acclaimed institution.</p>
<p><strong>Academic Calendar</strong><br />Participants may enroll for either the spring semester or the full year.&nbsp;<span>Approximate dates are provided based on past programs; students should expect them to vary by year. &nbsp;Please refer to the&nbsp;</span><span><a href="https://www.brown.edu/academics/college/special-programs/international-study/sites/brown.edu.academics.college.special-programs.international-study/files/uploads/Berlin%20Program%20Calendar%20Spring%202017_0.pdf" target="_blank">program calendar</a></span><span>&nbsp;for specific information about program dates.</span><strong>&nbsp;</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Full Year: September through late July/early August</li>
<li>Spring Semester Only: March through late July/early August</li>
</ul>
<h3>Housing</h3>
<p>Students may choose to apply for <a href="https://www.international.hu- berlin.de/en/studierende/aus-dem-ausland/accommodation-1?set_language=en" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">student university housing</a> through Humboldt University or live in a shared apartment with local German students. Students who opt for shared apartment living are initially housed in temporary housing arranged by the program for the orientation period.  Throughout the orientation period, students work with the on-site coordinator and a local housing agency to find a room in a shared  apartment, where they will live with local Germans for the  remainder of the semester or year.</p>
<h3>Eligibility and Requirements</h3>
<p><span>The Brown in Germany program in Berlin is open to Brown and visiting undergraduate students. &nbsp;</span></p>
<p><span></span>Students must be in good academic standing. &nbsp;Students must have completed Intermediate German I/GRMN 0300 (third-semester college-level German), or the equivalent (Intermediate level track) or Twentieth-Century German Culture/GRMN 0500 (fifth-semester college-level German language), or the equivalent (Advanced level track) before beginning studies in Germany. &nbsp;</p>
<h3>Application Instructions</h3>
<p>Apply by the<a href="http://www.brown.edu/academics/college/special-programs/international-study/student-resources/getting-started/application-deadlines" target="_blank"> deadline </a>using   Brown's online application system via the "apply now" and "return to   application" buttons on the right side of this page.&nbsp; Applications   typically require faculty recommendations so it is important to begin   the process early.</p>

                          <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Germany Program Costs 2016/17</h1>






<div class="clear-block">


<article id="node-1326" class="main-article node-page node">








  <div class="content">
        <p><strong>Germany Costs: 2016/17</strong></p>
<p>The fees, charges, and expenses shown are estimates only and subject to change. Spring semester is based on 5 months (March - July) and full year is based on 10 months (September - July).</p>
<table>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>Expense</td>
<td>Expense Type</td>
<td>Spring<br /></td>
<td>Year</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Program Fee*</td>
<td>Billed by Brown</td>
<td>$25112</td>
<td>$50224</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Brown Student Health Insurance&nbsp;** (waived if students&nbsp;demonstrate that&nbsp;they are covered by a comprehensive&nbsp;U.S.-based health insurance policy)&nbsp;</td>
<td>Billed by Brown</td>
<td>$2068 (available only for students who are newly enrolled at Brown for the S17 semester)</td>
<td>$3548 (annual rate)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Orientation Housing Fee (waived if student has arranged for university housing or for private housing before arrival in Berlin)</td>
<td>Billed by Brown</td>
<td>&nbsp;$650&nbsp;<span>estimate; actual final cost may vary&nbsp;</span></td>
<td>&nbsp;$650&nbsp;<span>estimate; actualy final cost may vary&nbsp;</span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Housing</td>
<td>Direct Student Cost</td>
<td>185 - 470 €/month for a university student residence.&nbsp;<span>T</span><span>he minimum rental period is 6 months:</span><span>&nbsp;</span><span>March 1 -&nbsp;</span><span>August 31</span>&nbsp;<span>OR approximately 2000 € for a room in a shared, privately-rented flat&nbsp;<span>(500 €/month is the market rate.)</span></span></td>
<td><span><span>185 - 470 €/month for a university student residence.&nbsp;</span><span>T</span><span>he minimum rental period is 6 months:</span><span>&nbsp;September 1 - February 28 for the fall semester and&nbsp;</span><span>March 1 -&nbsp;</span><span>August 31 for the spring semester</span><span>&nbsp;</span><span>OR approximately 5000 € for a room in a shared, privately-rented flat&nbsp;(500 €/month is the market rate.)</span></span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Airfare</td>
<td>Direct Student Cost</td>
<td>$1200</td>
<td>$1200</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Meals<span> </span></td>
<td>Direct Student Cos</td>
<td>approximately 2250 € (15 €/day)</td>
<td><span>approximately 4500 € (15 €/day)</span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Personal&nbsp;(estimate varies according to individual spending habits; does not include personal travel)</td>
<td>Direct Student Cost</td>
<td>$1700</td>
<td>$3060</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Books</td>
<td>Direct Student Cost</td>
<td>up to $200 (varies according to course of study)</td>
<td><span>up to $400 (varies according to course of study)</span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Required Immunizations and Medications (if applicable and not covered by insurance)</td>
<td>Direct Student Cost</td>
<td>n/a</td>
<td>n/a</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<p>*<strong>Program Fee</strong>: The program fee covers onsite tuition, including the orientation intensive language course, related academic programming, and program-sponsored excursions. It also covers the onsite orientation program, emergency travel assistance, semester-long student pass for public transportation, public transportaton pass for first month orientation/intensive language course, student residency permit fee, and the services of the onsite program staff. This program is an exchange program, and in order to facilitate cultural exchange and interaction, a portion of the program fee you pay supports exchange visitors to Brown from this site.</p>
<p><strong>**Health Insurance:</strong>&nbsp;Students will be billed automatically for Brown Student Health Insurance, unless an online “Health Insurance Waiver Form” is completed by June 1st for Fall and Full year programs or January 1st for Spring programs. You will receive a Student Health Insurance Plan information packet, which will include instructions on how to waive the insurance online if you have other comparable insurance in place.</p>
<p><strong>***Visa Fees:&nbsp;</strong>&nbsp;For some programs, students may need to apply for a visa or register upon arrival. Costs for processing of individual visas and registration range from $50 to $500, depending on the country and on the citizenship of the student.</p>
  </div>

                          <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Brown in Germany</h1>






<div class="clear-block">


<article id="node-288" class="main-article node-page node">








  <div class="content">
        <p><span class="thumbnail-wrapper inline inline-left" style="width:203px;"><img src="https://www.brown.edu/academics/college/special-programs/international-study/sites/brown.edu.academics.college.special-programs.international-study/files/images/brandenburg-gate-275437_0.preview.jpg" alt="" title=""  class="image image-preview " width="195" height="146" /></span><strong>At a Glance</strong><br />City: Berlin<br />Partner: <a href="http://www.hu-berlin.de/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">Humboldt University</a><br />Housing: University student housing or independent shared apartment with local German students<br />Language Requirements: <span>Third-semester college-level German for the Intermediate track or f</span>ifth-semester college-level German for the Advanced track (at Brown&nbsp;<a href="http://brown.edu/academics/college/special-programs/international-study/student-resources/getting-started/language-requirements#german">GRMN 0300 or GRMN 0500</a>)<br />Calendar: Full Year or Spring semester<br />Accepts Non-Brown Students: Yes</p>
<h3>Overview</h3>
<p>Imagine yourself in&nbsp;the lively capital city of Berlin, where you will find yourself immersed in&nbsp;Germany’s rich culture and complex history.&nbsp;Continuing to grapple with the effects of the 1989 reunification while experiencing steady growth of its ethnic minority population, Berlin offers an ideal venue to study the changing face of present-day Europe and a fascinating glimpse into&nbsp;Germany’s past, present and future.</p>
<h3>Orientation</h3>
<p>Brown's Berlin program begins a month-long orientation that includes a credit-bearing intensive German language course, cultural activities, and visits to different areas of the city.&nbsp;</p>
<p><em>Intensive German as a Foreign Language Course</em></p>
<p>All students enroll in an intensive month-long <a href="https://www.sprachenzentrum.hu-berlin.de/en/erasmus-deutsch-intensivkurse-en/march" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">German language course</a> offered by Humboldt University prior to the start of the regular semester courses. Students who successfully complete this course earn 7 <span>European Credit Transfer System (ECTS) credits, which is the equivalent of one Brown course credit.&nbsp;</span></p>
<p>The Advanced level track is a full immersion experience in which students take all of their courses in German at Humboldt  University. &nbsp;<span>Students in the Intermediate track may take advanced level courses in English at Humboldt University. Intermediate level students are required to enroll in a German language course during their regular semester, in additon to the intensive language course during orientation. &nbsp;</span></p>
<h3>Academics</h3>
<p>Following orientation, students enroll in regular semester courses at Humboldt University. Students must complete a minimum of &nbsp;20 - 23 ECTS&nbsp;<span>for evaluation through a <em>schein (</em>a document confirming that you have completed the course and received a letter grade), which, along with the intensive language course, would allow them to obtain a full semester credit equivalent at Brown.&nbsp;</span>Students may choose from Proseminare, Seminare (junior/senior level courses) or  Übungen (lab courses with field trips). Students are encouraged to  attend Vorlesungen (lecture courses in which there is no assessed work).&nbsp; Students may take a maximum of two Vorlesungen and may opt to enroll in one or both for credit. Students who wish to earn credit must arrange with the professor to be  given work on which a grade can be assessed. Please visit the <a href="https://www.hu-berlin.de/en/studies/counselling/course-catalogue" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">Humboldt University course catalog</a> for the most current list of course offerings.</p>
<p>Past program participants have studied history, art history, international studies, political science, religion, German, Medical Studies, natural sciences, chemistry, physics, computer science, comparative literarture, and international relations<span></span>. Students may enroll  in all departments of the universities for which they have appropriate  academic preparation.&nbsp;</p>
<p>In addition to academic programs, students have pursued internships at art galleries, the Aspen Institute, and the American embassy.&nbsp;</p>
<p><strong>Humboldt University</strong></p>
<p>Founded in 1810 by the Prussian king, Friedrich Wilhelm III, <a href="https://www.hu-berlin.de/en?set_language=en" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">Humboldt University</a>'s mission has long been to achieve a unity of teaching and research and provide students with an all-around humanist education. By drawing on and contributing to the rich history and cultural life of the city, Humboldt has remained true to its principles and become an internationally acclaimed institution.</p>
<p><strong>Academic Calendar</strong><br />Participants may enroll for either the spring semester or the full year.&nbsp;<span>Approximate dates are provided based on past programs; students should expect them to vary by year. &nbsp;Please refer to the&nbsp;</span><span><a href="https://www.brown.edu/academics/college/special-programs/international-study/sites/brown.edu.academics.college.special-programs.international-study/files/uploads/Berlin%20Program%20Calendar%20Spring%202017_0.pdf" target="_blank">program calendar</a></span><span>&nbsp;for specific information about program dates.</span><strong>&nbsp;</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Full Year: September through late July/early August</li>
<li>Spring Semester Only: March through late July/early August</li>
</ul>
<h3>Housing</h3>
<p>Students may choose to apply for <a href="https://www.international.hu- berlin.de/en/studierende/aus-dem-ausland/accommodation-1?set_language=en" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">student university housing</a> through Humboldt University or live in a shared apartment with local German students. Students who opt for shared apartment living are initially housed in temporary housing arranged by the program for the orientation period.  Throughout the orientation period, students work with the on-site coordinator and a local housing agency to find a room in a shared  apartment, where they will live with local Germans for the  remainder of the semester or year.</p>
<h3>Eligibility and Requirements</h3>
<p><span>The Brown in Germany program in Berlin is open to Brown and visiting undergraduate students. &nbsp;</span></p>
<p><span></span>Students must be in good academic standing. &nbsp;Students must have completed Intermediate German I/GRMN 0300 (third-semester college-level German), or the equivalent (Intermediate level track) or Twentieth-Century German Culture/GRMN 0500 (fifth-semester college-level German language), or the equivalent (Advanced level track) before beginning studies in Germany. &nbsp;</p>
<h3>Application Instructions</h3>
<p>Apply by the<a href="http://www.brown.edu/academics/college/special-programs/international-study/student-resources/getting-started/application-deadlines" target="_blank"> deadline </a>using   Brown's online application system via the "apply now" and "return to   application" buttons on the right side of this page.&nbsp; Applications   typically require faculty recommendations so it is important to begin   the process early.</p>

	<div id="for-global-travelers" class="clear-block block block-menu ">
	
	<h2>For Global Travelers</h2>	
		<div class="content"><ul class="menu"><li class="leaf first menu-controller039sofficetravelpoliciesandprocedures"><a href="http://brown.edu/about/administration/controller/accounts-payable/travel-management-real" title="">Controller&#039;s Office Travel Policies and Procedures </a></li>
<li class="leaf menu-internationalhealthinsurance"><a href="http://www.brown.edu/about/administration/insurance/international-travel-insurance/additional-health-insurance" title="">International Health Insurance </a></li>
<li class="leaf menu-registerwithbrown039sinternationalsosservice"><a href="https://www.internationalsos.com/MasterPortal/default.aspx?membnum=11BSGC000031&amp;akey=F3429D05-ED1D-B594-D93BA255D50416CB" title="">Register with Brown&#039;s International SOS Service</a></li>

				<h1><a href="/academics/german-studies/" title="German Studies" accesskey="2"><span class="site-title">German Studies</span></a></h1><div id="site-slogan" class="slogan banner-subtext"></div>			</div></section>

                  <div class="columns no-right-column">
        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Graduate Students</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
                                                  <div class="clear-block"> 
<article id="node-193" class="main-article node">








  <div class="content">
        <p>&nbsp;</p>
<table>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><span class="thumbnail-wrapper inline inline-left" style="width:108px;"><img src="https://www.brown.edu/academics/german-studies/sites/brown.edu.academics.german-studies/files/images/slgalassophoto.thumbnail.JPG" alt="" title=""  class="image image-thumbnail " width="100" height="100" /></span></td>
<td><a href="http://brown.edu/academics/german-studies/eric-foster-bio"></a>
<p><strong><a href="http://brown.edu/academics/german-studies/stephanie-galasso-bio-0">STEPHANIE GALASSO</a></strong> joined the German Studies department in Fall 2012, after graduating from the University of California at Davis in June 2012 with degrees in German and English Literature. Her dissertation explores the interrelations between and among conceptions of genre and national identity in German romantic literature, particularly in the works of Bettine von Arnim, Karoline von Günderrode, J.W. von Goethe, and Friedrich Hölderlin. Her research draws on and expands German feminist scholarship by critiquing depictions of perceived cultural otherness at both the thematic and formal levels. Portions of her dissertation work were completed at the Universität Konstanz on a Fulbright research fellowship from 2015-2016.&nbsp;</p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="mailto:rebecca_haubrich@brown.edu" rel="nofollow"><span class="thumbnail-wrapper inline inline-left" style="width:108px;"><img src="https://www.brown.edu/academics/german-studies/sites/brown.edu.academics.german-studies/files/images/Rebecca Haubrich pic.thumbnail.jpg" alt="" title=""  class="image image-thumbnail " width="100" height="100" /></span></a></td>
<td>
<p><strong><a href="mailto:rebecca_haubrich@brown.edu" rel="nofollow">REBECCA HAUBRICH</a></strong> <span><span style="color:#000000; vertical-align:baseline; background-color:transparent">Rebecca Haubrich received an M.A. in Comparative Literature and German Studies from the Goethe University of Frankfurt (Main) in 2011. In her thesis, she focused on the structural metamorphoses of poetic language from Ovid's artist-myths to their lyrical transformations of the nineteenth and twentieth century. In her dissertation, she plans to explore the relationship between the work of mourning and aesthetics, specifically in writings on psychoanalysis and photography. Before coming to Brown, she was a Visiting Research Scholar at Yale University (2012/13).</span></span></p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span class="thumbnail-wrapper inline inline-left" style="width:108px;"><img src="https://www.brown.edu/academics/german-studies/sites/brown.edu.academics.german-studies/files/images/Daniel Lange-1.thumbnail.jpg" alt="" title=""  class="image image-thumbnail " width="100" height="100" /></span></td>
<td>
<p><a href="mailto:DANIEL_LANGE@BROWN.EDU" rel="nofollow"><strong>DANIEL LANGE</strong></a> received his degrees in Comparative Literature, International Women's and Gender History (both 2015), and Romance Studies (2013) from the University of Vienna. He has a particular interest in post-war German and Austrian literature (Aichinger, Mayröcker, Kling), translation and etymology, as well as their cultural implications.</p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span class="thumbnail-wrapper inline inline-left" style="width:108px;"><img src="https://www.brown.edu/academics/german-studies/sites/brown.edu.academics.german-studies/files/images/person-thumbnail.thumbnail.jpg" alt="" title=""  class="image image-thumbnail " width="100" height="100" /></span></td>
<td>
<p><strong><a href="mailto:jasmin_meier1@brown.edu" rel="nofollow">JASMIN MEIER</a> </strong>studied philosophy, German Studies (BA) and the Ethics of Textual Cultures (MA) in an interdisciplinary degree program that brings together philosophy, German literary studies, linguistics, and religious studies. In her MA thesis, she focused on concepts of hope in Friedrich Nietzsche’s work. Her main current research focus continues to be the work of Nietzsche and its reception—often the subject of misuse or even abuse—in the present and the past. Her perspective derives from an “ethical” point of view and poses questions such as the following: What does it mean to read in an “ethical” manner, and what does this practice exclude? What does the possibility of “unethical” reading tell us about language itself?</p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span class="thumbnail-wrapper inline inline-left" style="width:108px;"><img src="https://www.brown.edu/academics/german-studies/sites/brown.edu.academics.german-studies/files/images/Christian Obst-1.thumbnail.jpg" alt="" title=""  class="image image-thumbnail " width="100" height="100" /></span></td>
<td>
<p><a href="mailto:CHRISTIAN_OBST@BROWN.EDU" rel="nofollow"><strong>CHRISTIAN OBST</strong></a> studied Comparative Literature, Philosophy, and Sociology at the Goethe University in Frankfurt am Main, completing his studies with an M.A. thesis exploring the implied theory and politics of metaphor in the writings of Karl Marx. The authors who have informed his research are Hegel, Adorno, Derrida, Blanchot, and Benjamin, along with Musil. Obst, who also spent a year as a visiting scholar at Yale’s German Department, is interested in the ways in which various kinds of texts (literary, philosophical, theological, and essayistic) can be investigated in the medium of their literariness. He is preparing a project that examines the problematic nexus of coinage and characteristic marks in Benjamin and the Book of Esther.</p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span class="thumbnail-wrapper inline inline-left" style="width:108px;"><img src="https://www.brown.edu/academics/german-studies/sites/brown.edu.academics.german-studies/files/images/photo.thumbnail.jpg" alt="" title=""  class="image image-thumbnail " width="100" height="100" /></span></td>
<td>
<p><strong><a href="mailto:michael_paninski@brown.edu" rel="nofollow">MICHAEL PANINSKI</a></strong> studied Theater, Film and Media Studies, German, and Philosophy at the University of Vienna, where he received his M.A. in 2015 with a thesis entitled <em>Hermeneutics Of Failure – Underway to Bertolt Brecht’s </em>Fatzer<em>-fragment</em>. The text analyzes the possibilities of re-reading, re-discovering and re-interpreting the works of Walter Benjamin and Bertolt Brecht in the aftermath of the theoretical conceptions of fragment, detail and discontinuity. Michael Paninski's research interests range from the legacy of the fragmentary demand of the Romantic via the broad operational areas of Critical Theory to current questions in literary theory and continental philosophy. At Brown University he wishes to explore contemporary discourses and narratives of human rights, justice, and violence in the wake of deconstructive theories of literature and language. His questions circle around the incalculable element within language—a critical residual, remnant, or relic (<em>Rest</em>)—and around the question of what happens when the intervention of this <em>Other</em> is not excluded from the calculus or economy of the political.</p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span class="thumbnail-wrapper inline inline-left" style="width:108px;"><img src="https://www.brown.edu/academics/german-studies/sites/brown.edu.academics.german-studies/files/images/M Paninski.thumbnail.jpg" alt="" title=""  class="image image-thumbnail " width="100" height="100" /></span></td>
<td>
<p><strong><a href="mailto:mirjam_paninski@brown.edu" rel="nofollow">MIRJAM PANINSKI</a></strong> <span>studied Comparative Literature, German Studies, Aesthetics, and the Philosophy of Culture at the University of Vienna. Her research interests include translation and translatability of and within 20<sup>th</sup> century poetry, the <em>gaps </em>of language, and multilingual literature by authors such as Ilse Aichinger, Ingeborg Bachmann, Yoko Tawada and Gertrude Stein.</span></p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span class="thumbnail-wrapper inline inline-left" style="width:108px;"><img src="https://www.brown.edu/academics/german-studies/sites/brown.edu.academics.german-studies/files/images/Miriam Rainer-6.thumbnail.JPG" alt="" title=""  class="image image-thumbnail " width="100" height="100" /></span></td>
<td>
<p><a href="mailto:MIRIAM_RAINER@BROWN.EDU" rel="nofollow"><strong>MIRIAM RAINER </strong></a>received her B.A. in American Studies with a minor in German Studies from the University of Hamburg in 2010 and her M.A. in Comparative Literature from the University of Vienna in 2014. In her M.A. thesis <em>Zögern / Hesitate</em>,<em> </em>she traces the movement of translating and its manifestation in theoretical and literary texts (Walter Benjamin, Paul Celan, Peter Waterhouse, Rosmarie Waldrop). She is co-founder of the collaborative translation project VERSATORIUM. Her research interests include the affiliation between the theory and practice of literary translation, language philosophy, philology, and critical theory.</p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span class="thumbnail-wrapper inline inline-left" style="width:108px;"><img src="https://www.brown.edu/academics/german-studies/sites/brown.edu.academics.german-studies/files/images/Jan Tabor pic.thumbnail.jpeg" alt="" title=""  class="image image-thumbnail " width="100" height="100" /></span></td>
<td>
<p><span><strong><a href="mailto:jan_georg_tabor@brown.edu" rel="nofollow">JAN GEORG TABOR</a></strong>&nbsp; studied German and Philosophy (B.A., 2012), as well as Ethics of Textual Cultures (M.A., 2015) at the Friedrich-Alexander-University Erlangen-Nuremberg and the Charles University in Prague. His often crossdisciplinary research interests include literary theory, critical historiography, and institutional sociology/critique. He also is interested in ways of (re)thinking and expanding comparativism. His main fields of investigation have been German Idealism (particularly Hegel) and literature of the 19th and early 20th centuries.</span></p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td></td>
<td></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<p class="small"><strong style="color:#2f2a20">Graduate Students from Other Departments</strong></p>
<p class="small">There are many other graduate students at Brown working  towards Ph.D.s in other field but who are active in our department  through their participation in seminars, workshops, and colloquia.</p>
<p class="small">NATALIE ADLER (Comparative Literature)<br />Natalie received her B.A. from the Gallatin School of  Individualized Study at NYU. Before coming to Brown, she was an English  teacher at a primary school in Besançon, France. She studies modernism  and psychoanalysis.</p>
<p class="small">FILIP METRO ANCHIDIM ANI (History)<br /> BA in History and Spanish Language and Literature; MA in History (U.  Alberta). Filip works on modern war, genocide, interethnic communal massacre, and the Holocaust in the Department of History. Filip has an MA in Comparative Literature from Brown University, where he worked on representations of modern violence in the poetry and literature of the European tradition.</p>
<p class="small">PETER KIM (English)<br />Peter holds a B.A. in English from UC Berkeley. He is  interested in the relations between literature and philosophy with  regards to critical theory and 19th-Century British literature. Specific  interests include Frankfurt School aesthetics, the Victorian political  novel, and theories of English and German Romanticism.</p>
<p class="small">NATALIE LOZINSKI-VEACH  (Comparative Literature)<br />Natalie holds a B.A. in English with a minor in Spanish and a  B.A. in German, both from SUNY New Paltz. She is interested in  animality, posthumanism, feminism, theories of embodiment, reciprocity  and liminality and their relationship to textual bodies and language.  She works on 20th and 21st century literature, with a focus on German  and Polish socialist and post-socialist works.</p>
<p class="small">ADAM J SACKS (History)<br />Adam  holds an M.A. from Brown University, a Masters of  Science from the City College of the City University of New York, and a  B.A.  Summa Cum Laude  from Cornell University. In 2011, he was the  Cahnmann Foundation Fellow at the Center for Jewish History in New York  and was awarded the dissertation Grant of the Central European History  Society. He is serving as a fellow of the Studienstiftung des Deutschen  Volkes in 2012 while also a Guest Researcher at the new Reseach Center  for Exile Culture at the Universitaet der Kuenste in Berlin.</p>
<p class="small">IAN SAMPSON (English)<br />Ian  is a doctoral student in the English department,  studying Romantic poetry and posthumanist theory. He holds degrees in  English and creative writing from the University of Calgary and Simon  Fraser University, where he wrote an MA thesis about the American  sinologist Ernest Fenollosa and his influence on Ezra Pound. Since  coming to Brown in 2011, he has also become a clandestine Germanist,  reading Hölderlin and Heidegger alongside Wordsworth and Whitehead – a  side-effect, no doubt, of regularly summering with family in Köln.  Beyond Romanticism, his interests include media theory, psychoanalysis,  and the avant-garde.</p>
<p class="small">JONATHAN SOZEK (Religion and Critical Thought)<br />Jon received a B.A. in liberal arts from Sarah Lawrence  College in 2003 and an M.A. (honours) in religious studies at McGill  University in 2006.  After working for several years in secondary  education, Jon moved to Belgium to complete a second B.A. (2009) and  M.A. (2010) in philosophy at the Katholieke Universiteit Leuven.  His  ongoing reserarch interests include the conceptual histories of  'religion' and 'the secular' and of their relation, modern theories of  myth and the politics of mythmaking, political theology, and critical  theory.</p>
<p class="small">SETH THORN (Multimedia &amp; Electronic Music Experiments)<br />Seth             studied philosophy, critical theory and viola performance at  Northwestern and  completed an M.A. in political theory at the  Goethe-Universität in Frankfurt am Main.  In addition to his doctoral  pursuits in Brown's Multimedia and Electronic Music Experiments (aka  "MEME") program, Seth has special emphasis on the study of German  philosophy and the philosophy of music and sound where it appears in  that tradition and is working towards an M.A. in German Studies.</p>
<p class="small"><span><span>GEOFFREY WILDANGER (Comparative Literature)</span><br /><span>Geoffrey Wildanger works at the confluence of art history, literary history, and critical theory. Prior to beginning his PhD at Brown, he received a Master's at the University of California, Davis, and was a Helena Rubinstein Fellow at the Whitney Independent Study Program in New York. He recently published the article "Catastrophic Affects" in the British journal&nbsp;</span><em>Transmission Annual</em><span>.</span>&nbsp;</span></p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p class="small"><span><br /></span></p>

				<h1><a href="/academics/german-studies/" title="German Studies" accesskey="2"><span class="site-title">German Studies</span></a></h1><div id="site-slogan" class="slogan banner-subtext"></div>			</div></section>

                  <div class="columns no-right-column">
        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Courses in German Studies</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
                                                  <div class="clear-block"> 
<article id="node-215" class="main-article node">








  <div class="content">
        <p>The Department of German Studies offers a set of courses that covers the range from beginning language instruction to graduate seminars.</p>
<p>You can select either "Fall" or "Spring" in the navigation menu to the left to see our current offerings.</p>
<p>Alternatively, you can download PDFs of both current and past offerings here:</p>

				<h1><a href="/academics/german-studies/" title="German Studies" accesskey="2"><span class="site-title">German Studies</span></a></h1><div id="site-slogan" class="slogan banner-subtext"></div>			</div></section>

        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Fall Courses</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
                                                  <div class="clear-block"> 
<article id="node-220" class="main-article node">








  <div class="content">
        <h4>Primarily for Undergraduates<strong><br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; </strong></h4>
<h4></h4>
<p><strong>GRMN0100&nbsp;&nbsp; Beginning German&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Jane Sokolosky, M Paninski, J Tabor, C Obst</strong><br />A course in the language and cultures of German-speaking countries. Four hours per week plus regular computer and listening comprehension work. At the end of the year, students will be able to communicate successfully about everyday topics. This is the first half of a year-long course whose first semester grade is normally a temporary one. Neither semester may be elected independently without special written permission. The final grade submitted at the end of the course work in GRMN 0200 covers the entire year and is recorded as the final grade for both semesters. Students who have a conflict with the Tuesday hour should contact the instructor.<strong><br /> </strong><strong>S01&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; MWF &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; 9-9:50,&nbsp;&nbsp; T 12-12:50&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; MWF&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; 190 Hope 203; T JWalter Wilson 403<br />S02&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; MWF &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; 11-11:50, T 12-12:50&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; MTWF&nbsp; J Walter Wilson 201<br />S03&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; MWF&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; 12-12:50, T 12-12:50&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; MWF&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; 190 Hope 203; T JWW 501<br />S04&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; MWF &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; 1-1:50, T 12-12:50&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; MWF&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; 190 Hope 203<br /></strong></p>
<p><strong>GRMN0300&nbsp;&nbsp; Intermediate German I&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Jane Sokolosky/Daniel Lange</strong><br />Focuses on deepening students' understanding of modern German culture by reading texts and viewing films pertinent to Germany today. Intended to provide a thorough review of German grammar and help students develop their writing, reading, listening, and speaking skills. Frequent writing assignments. Four hours per week. Recommended prerequisite: GRMN 0200. Students who have a conflict with the Thursday hour should contact the instructor.<strong><br />S01&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; MWF 10-10:50, Th 12-12:50&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; MWThF&nbsp;&nbsp; 190 Hope 203<br />S02&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; MWF 1-1:50, Th 12-12:50&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; MWThF&nbsp;&nbsp; Barus &amp; Holley 163<br /></strong></p>
<p><strong>GRMN0500F&nbsp; 20<sup>th</sup> Century German Culture&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Kristina Mendicino </strong><strong>&nbsp;</strong>A broad exploration of twentieth-century German culture using many kinds of written and visual texts (e.g. literature, journalism, film, art). While continuing to work on all four language skills (speaking, listening, reading, writing) students will gain more intensive knowledge about German culture, society, and history. In German. Recommended prerequisite: GRMN 0400. WRIT<strong><br />S01&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; MWF 11-11:50&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; 190 Hope 203<br /></strong></p>
<p><strong>GRMN0750F&nbsp; Historical Crime Fiction&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Thomas Kniesche</strong> <br />There is almost no time period that has not been  covered by historical crime fiction. From ancient Egypt and Rome to 18th  century China, historical crime fiction has complemented and contested  our knowledge of history. In this seminar, we will do some extensive  time travel and explore how crime fiction explores the past and  challenges our understanding of bygone times. Readings of texts by Ellis  Peters, Umberto Eco, Peter Tremayne, Lindsey Davis, Alan Gordon, Robert  van Gulik, Laura Rowland, among others. LILE FYS WRIT<strong><br /> S01&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; TuTh 2:30-3:50&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; John Hay Library 315<br /></strong></p>
<p><strong>GRMN1320C&nbsp;&nbsp; Goethe's Children &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Dennis Johannssen</strong><br />The name “Goethe” looms large over modern German literature, but what are founding figures without the daughters and sons that question and challenge their authority? This course offers a broad introduction to Goethe’s life and works, focusing on themes and questions of youth and childhood in his writings. Readings include canonical works such as <em>The Sorrows of Young Werther</em>, <em>Iphigenia in Tauris</em>, <em>Wilhelm Meister’s Apprenticeship</em>, <em>Faust</em>, and <em>Elective Affinities</em>. We will also examine Goethe’s legacy by looking at musical, theatrical, and philosophical responses to images of youth and childhood in his works. [Taught in German; all students welcome.]<strong><br />S01&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; TuTh 2:30-3:50 &nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp; 190 Hope 203</strong><strong> </strong></p>
<p><strong><strong>GRMN1320O Freudian Inspirations:&nbsp; Psychoanalysis and the Arts&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Benjamin Brand<br /> </strong></strong>This course engages with the central concepts and motifs of Freud’s psychoanalytic theory and how they relate to works of literature, art, and film. What do Sophocles, Ovid, and Hoffmann tell us when we read <em>Oedipus Rex</em>, <em>The Metamorphoses</em>, and <em>The Sandman</em> today? How does our understanding of these texts differ from or resemble Freud’s reading of them? And when we engage art that cites psychoanalytic concepts—such as Hitchcock’s <em>Marnie</em> or Lars von Trier’s <em>Melancholia</em>—do we recognize the Freud we encounter in our readings of his own texts? (In English; all students welcome)<br /><strong>S01&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; MWF 10-10:50am&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Metacalf Research Bldg 104<br /></strong></p>
<p><strong>GRMN1440G Talking Animals and the Politics of World Literature&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Christine Ivanovic<br /> </strong>We have encountered talking animals within literature for over four thousand years. Alongside the Fables of Aesop, there are comparable animal stories from Ancient Babylon, Egypt, and from the Indian-Arabian-Persian cultural space, whose distribution was just as far-reaching. Animal fables appear easy to understand and universally valid. But do we really understand what it means when animals talk in texts? This course will employ current approaches to human-animal-studies alongside concepts of world literature to examine why animals are made to talk, what functions animal stories have, and what the conditions for their dissemination are. [In English; all Brown students welcome.]<br /> <strong>S01&nbsp;&nbsp; TuTh 4-5:30pm&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Sayles Hall 014<br /> </strong></p>
<p><strong>GRMN1440S&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Grimms' Fairy Tales</strong> &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <strong>Kristina Mendicino</strong><br />“One doesn't know the sorts of things one has in one's house," says the servant girl in Kafka's "A Country Doctor," as a stranger emerges from an unused sty who will incorporate the doctor’s own most intimate—and violent—desires. Finding more than one seeks in one's midst is among the core motifs of the Grimms' “Household Tales” that we will trace, following how they move writers of and on literature, psychoanalysis, critical theory, gender, nation, and race. Reading the Grimms, we may find: what was "once upon a time" is not finished, nor can these uncanny tales be domesticated.&nbsp; In English.<strong><br />S01&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; MWF 2-2:50&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Smith Buonanno G18<br /></strong></p>
<p><strong>GRMN1<strong>440U&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <strong>Migrantenliteratur</strong></strong> &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Thomas Kniesche</strong><br />Literature written by migrants and their descendants has become a vital part of post-war German language and culture. Since the arrival of the so-called guest workers in the 1950s, the Jewish immigrants from the former Soviet Union during the 1990s, and recent Syrian refugees, Germany has become an “Einwanderungsland” (a country of immigration) and an increasingly multicultural society. This has triggered anxieties and phobias, exploited by populist politicians and movements, but it has also profoundly changed the literary landscape. The class will explore the contributions of authors such as Rafik Schami, Feridun Zaimoglu, Emine Özdamar, Zafer Senocak, and others.&nbsp; Taught in German.<strong><br />S01&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; TuTh&nbsp; 10:30-11:50</strong><strong>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; 190 Hope 203<br /></strong></p>
<p><strong>GRMN1440X &nbsp;&nbsp; Stranger Things:&nbsp; The German Novella &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Natalie Lozinski-Veach</strong><br />Goethe’s famous description of the novella as an “unheard-of event” holds true to this day:&nbsp;scandals, murder, and the supernatural abound in this seminal German genre. Both meticulously structured and notoriously difficult to define, the novella as a form mirrors the paradoxes of its narratives. In this course, we will ask how form and content come together in the novella to engender strange occurrences that vacillate between everyday experiences and fever dreams. What is it about the German novella that creates such a particular sense of unease, and how does this genre mediate modern experience? [In English. All students welcome.]<br /><strong>S01&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; MWF 12-12:50&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; J Walter Wilson 201<br /></strong></p>
<p><strong>GRMN1660Q&nbsp; Film and the Third Reich&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Dennis Johannssen</strong><br />This course explores the cinema of the Third Reich as well as filmic responses to World War II and the Holocaust. Sections will be dedicated to propaganda films by Leni Riefenstahl and others; to the relationship between Third Reich cinema and Hollywood; to propaganda films produced by the Allied forces, and to movies about the Holocaust such as <em>Shoah </em>and<em> Schindler’s List</em>. We will discuss key concepts of film theory, cinema’s political efficacy, Holocaust representation in film, music, and language, and questions of trauma, commemoration, and victimhood. [Taught in English; students from diverse fields welcome.]<strong><br />S01&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; TuTh 9-10:20 &nbsp;&nbsp; 190 Hope 203</strong><strong> </strong></p>
<p><strong><strong>SWED0100&nbsp; Beginning Swedish&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Ann Weinstein<br /> </strong></strong>Swedish  100 is an introduction to both Sweden and Swedish, covering various  aspects of Swedish history, art and society, as well as screening at  least three Swedish films per semester. The course packet contains the  text/workbook, <em>Mål 1</em>, with additional materials. We will cover one chapter of <em>Mål</em> per week, with quizzes every three weeks. There will be a midterm and a  final exam, along with a short take-home project.&nbsp; This is a small  class, so your presence is absolutely required. Emphasis will be placed  on <em>speaking</em> and <em>understanding </em>Swedish. Good will and good humor are required.<strong><br /> <strong>S01&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; TuTh 4-5:30&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; 190 Hope 102<br /></strong></strong></p>
<h2></h2>
<h4></h4>
<h4></h4>
<h4><strong>Primarily for Graduates<br /></strong></h4>
<p><strong>GRMN2661F&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Textual Border Crossings:&nbsp; Translational Literature &nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Christine Ivanovic</strong><br /> We will first attempt to discover what happens to a translation, as well as to the translator, when a text asks for asylum in the guest house of another language: “domestication” or “foreignization,” as Lawrence Venuti puts it? Or is it appropriation, acculturation, adoption? Or rather estrangement, alienation, defamiliarization? Next we will investigate different models of derivative, parasitic, and translational writing by authors as diverse as J. Franzen, E. Fried, Ch. Hawkey, E. Jelinek, Y. Tawada, P. Waterhouse, and others. Finally, we will examine translational writing as a means of decolonizing world literature.<br /> <strong>S01&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; W 3-5:30pm&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Salomon Center 203</strong></p>
<p><strong>GRMN2661K &nbsp;&nbsp; Thinking Tradition &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Gerhard Richter</strong><br />Our modes of being in the world, along with our languages, institutions, and most fundamental assumptions and practices, are determined by the dead who preceeded us. Through close readings of key texts that address the rich and vexed concept of “tradition”—Arendt’s <em>Between Past and Future</em>, relevant passages from Heidegger’s <em>Being and Time</em>, Adorno’s “On Tradition” and pertinent sections from <em>Negative Dialectics</em>—we will address issues of fundamental significance to critical thought today. To interrogate the concept of tradition, we also will attempt to understand the stakes of Arendt’s and Adorno’s fundamentally divergent interpretations of Benjamin’s philosophy of history.&nbsp; [Seminar will be taught in English. Graduate Students from diverse fields welcome.]<br /><strong>S01&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; M 3-5:30&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; 111 Thayer, Watson Institute, 138<br /></strong></p>
<p><strong>&nbsp;</strong><span style="text-decoration:underline"> &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; </span><strong>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; </strong></p>
<p><strong>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Courses Offered Beyond German Studies that May Count Towards the Concentration</strong></p>
<p><strong>COLT1210: Introduction to the Theory of Literature</strong> <br /> An historical introduction to problems of literary theory from the classical to the postmodern. Issues to be examined include mimesis, rhetoric, hermeneutics, history, psychoanalysis, formalisms and ideological criticism (questions of race, gender, sexuality, postcolonialism). Primarily for advanced undergraduates. Lectures, discussions; several short papers.<br /> Susan Bernstein, Karen Newman&nbsp;&nbsp; S01 MWF 1pm-1:50pm <br /> <strong><br /> ENGL1511X Capital and Culture<br /> </strong>An introduction to the literature and culture of the Victorian period through the categories and questions of political economy: especially the making of the working class, finance, and industrialization. The objective is twofold: to examine novelists, poets, and prose writers in the light of Karl Marx's influential analysis of capitalist society, but also to contextualize and situate Marx as a Victorian, who lived in London for the majority of his life.<br />Benjamin Parker &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; S01 MWF 9am-9:50am<strong><br /> </strong></p>
<p><strong>ENGL1761Q: W.G. Sebald and Some Interlocutors<br /> </strong>The works of W. G. Sebald have received a huge amount of critical attention since his death in 2001, particularly from critics interested in the question of the ethics of literature after Auschwitz. But what is Sebald’s literary heritage, and who are his interlocutors? What internal and external connections do his works establish? Besides Sebald’s works, readings will include Stendhal, Kafka, Walser, Borges, Bergson, Resnais, Lanzmann. Banner registrations after classes begin require instructor approval. Enrollment limited to 20. Not open to first-year students.<strong><br /> </strong>Timothy RT Bewes&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp; S01 TTh 2:30pm-3:50pm</p>
<p><strong>HIST1230B:</strong> <strong>Modern European Intellectual and Cultural History: The Fin de Siecle, 1880-1914</strong><br /> A sequel to HIST 1230A focusing on radical intellectual and cultural currents that challenged and destabilized the assumptions of Victorian high culture during the fin de siecle. Through a careful reading of primary texts by Hobhouse, Nietzsche, Weber, and Freud. The course explores issues such as the rise of mass consumer culture, neoliberal and neofascist politics, philosophic irrationalism, psychoanalysis, and the woman question. WRIT<br /> Mary Gluck S01 MWF 12pm-12:50pm</p>
<p>&nbsp;<strong>HIST1240A Politics of Violence in 20<sup>th</sup> Century Europe</strong><br />Europe's 20th century saw the emergence of forms of violence unthinkable in a world without mass politics. To better understand the changes in European states and societies that gave rise to total war and the violence associated with totalizing ideologies such as fascism and communism, we will read Lenin, Mussolini, Hitler, Fanon and others who sought to interpret violence as an extension of ideology. We will also read selections from more recent works by state leaders, historians and cultural figures from Ukraine to France, from Turkey to Great Britain who have reinterpreted past violence for present political ends.<strong><br /> </strong>Holly Case&nbsp; S01 TTh 10:30am-11:50am</p>
<p><strong>JUDS1820: Holocaust Literature<br /> </strong>Readings in works of prose and poetry by victims and survivors of the Holocaust that portray experiences in ghettos, in concentration camps, and in hiding. Additional readings in works of the post-war era by survivors and their offspring. Discussion of the moral, psychological, religious, and cultural dimensions of the Holocaust and its ongoing impact on humanity. WRIT<strong><br /> </strong>David Jacobson&nbsp;&nbsp; S01 MWF 11am-11:50am</p>
<p><strong>MUSC1500A: Major Masters and Repertoires of Music: Bach<br /> </strong>An examination of the life and work of Bach, including its placce in German church music, views of his contemporaries and explanation of hi manuscript and publishing history.<strong><br /> </strong>Louis Jodry&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; S01&nbsp;&nbsp; TTh 9am-10:20am<br /> <strong><br /> PHIL0050: Aesthetics: Art and Morality<br /> </strong>From Plato to the present, the power of the arts to trigger powerful emotions has been seen by some thinkers as a threat to morality, by others as a vital support. This debate raises such issues as whether aesthetic experience is a distinctive kind of experience and whether the creation and reception of art are autonomous activities free from the constraints of morality and politics. Beyond Plato, authors to be read will include such figures as Hume, Mendelssohn, Rousseau, Kant, Schiller, Hegel, Schopenhauer, Ruskin, Tolstoy, Collingwood, Stanley Cavell, Martha Nussbaum, Alexander Nehamas, and others.<br /> Paul D Guyer&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; S01 TTh 1pm-2:20pm</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><strong>&nbsp;</strong></p>
  </div>

				<h1><a href="/academics/german-studies/" title="German Studies" accesskey="2"><span class="site-title">German Studies</span></a></h1><div id="site-slogan" class="slogan banner-subtext"></div>			</div></section>
